> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=24)** Now, what's the obvious question?
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=26)** Why?
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=28)** Why did it drop from 18.1% down to 12.1%?
>
> **[0:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=32)** Well, we can answer that question, again, by using the DuPont framework.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=36)** As you can see here, we have the DuPont framework for 2013 and 2012.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=43)** We've got Target's performance broken down by profitability, efficiency, and leverage.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=49)** Let's just take a look at these comparisons and see what we can conclude.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=52)** First of all, let's look at leverage.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=53)** In 2012, their leverage was 2.9.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=57)** In other words, they had 2.9 dollars in assets for every dollar's worth of equity.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=63)** In 2013, that had dropped down to 2.7.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=67)** You'll notice that the assets between 2012 and 2013 dropped significantly a couple of billion dollars.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=75)** Part of the reason for that was Target decided to get out of the credit card business.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=80)** They used to have their own credit card in-house.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=84)** In 2013, they got out of that business, causing their assets to go down.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=89)** With those assets, they used 75% of 'em to pay down liabilities.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=95)** So, part of the reason for the decrease in their leverage, they reduced their assets and took some of that money to reduce their liabilities, pay down their liabilities.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=104)** So their leverage went down just a little bit from 2.9 to 2.7.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=109)** When it comes to efficiency, we see that between 2012 and 2013, Target actually got more efficient at generating sales with its assets, from 1.5 up to 1.6 in 2013.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=123)** So, we know the reason for decline was not because they were less efficient with using their asset, they were, in fact, more efficient.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=132)** When we look at the third ratio of the third component of return on equity, profitability, we see that's where Target got hit.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=139)** In 2012, their return on sales was 4.1%.
>
> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=145)** In 2013, it was 2.7%.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=147)** So, was the issue leverage?
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=150)** A little bit.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=151)** Efficiency?
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=153)** Not at all.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=154)** Profitability from 4.1% to 2.7%?
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=158)** A significant difference there.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=160)** What we can conclude from our DuPont analysis, comparing 2012 and 2013, is that over that period of time, Target had a little less leverage, and they were less profitable.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=174)** Those two items combine to account for their return on equity dropping from 18.1% down to 12.1%.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=182)** Now, just to make it interesting, let's compare Target in 2013 with Walmart in 2013.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=189)** You can see that Walmart's return on equity in 2013 was 19.7.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=194)** Target's was 12.1%.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=197)** The obvious question, why the difference?
>
> **[3:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=199)** What is Walmart doing so much better than Target?
>
> **[3:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=202)** Well, again, we can break return on equity down into its three components, profitability, efficiency, and leverage.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=209)** We note that Target actually has a little more leverage than does Walmart, 2.7 compared to 2.5.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=217)** When it comes to efficiency, we see Walmart's big secret.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=220)** They just are very good at generating sales with the assets that they have.
>
> **[3:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=227)** Walmart generated 2.3 dollars worth of sales for every dollar's worth of assets.
>
> **[3:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=233)** Contrast that with Target, which was able to generate a dollar and 60 cents worth of sales for each dollar worth of assets.
>
> **[4:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=241)** Efficiency is what Walmart is very good at, but we're not done yet, there's a third ratio, profitability.
>
> **[4:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=247)** What we can conclude from our analysis is that Walmart has a higher return on equity than Target, but the question is, why?
>
> **[4:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=256)** And the DuPont framework with it's three separate ratios allows us to pinpoint, why the difference.
>
> **[4:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=263)** In this case, Walmart is just more efficient and more profitable than is Target.
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/dupont-framework-target-and-walmart-2?u=76281980&t=268)** That's step one in our four-step process of financial ratio analysis.

#### Problem description to explanation
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=0)** - Okay, we've now completed step one, using the DuPont framework to break down return on equity into its component parts.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=7)** Now what?
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=9)** Well, it turns out the numbers can tell you what has happened.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=13)** Additional analysis can shed light on why things happened.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=17)** The numbers, as we're going to find, seldom provide the answers to questions.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=22)** The numbers point you in the direction of the next question.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=25)** And eventually we're going to end up talking to an individual.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=29)** So, to review, the DuPont framework analysis is the first step.
>
> **[0:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=33)** We do that first.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=35)** We take return on equity, break it down into profitability, efficiency, and leverage.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=40)** The second step in our analysis is to prepare common-size financial statements.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=45)** Common-size financial statements allow us to compare companies of different sizes, both across time or at the same point in time, across companies.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=53)** Based on that analysis, we then have additional ratios that we can use.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=58)** We have profitability ratios, efficiency ratios, and leverage ratios.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=63)** As we saw with the Walmart versus Target, we had issues about efficiency.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=69)** Well, we can then go analyze individual assets to see which assets are generating more sales.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=76)** With which assets are we more efficient?
>
> **[1:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=78)** There are ratios that'll point us in that direction.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=80)** But again, the first three steps are simply doing analysis with available information to lead us to the next clue.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=89)** We drill down a little more and a little more.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=91)** For example, we didn't need to drill down with leverage, because we saw that Target was more highly levered than was Walmart.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=100)** We don't have additional questions there.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=102)** But if we did, there are ratios related to leverage that we could look at.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=106)** All of this analysis eventually leads us to the right person who can tell us why are we different from our competitor, or why are we different from last year.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=117)** We do this analysis to get us to the right person, to ask the right question.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/problem-description-to-explanation?u=76281980&t=123)** That's where we're headed.

> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/olympic-medals-and-gdp-per-capita?u=76281980&t=108)** The United States comes in at number six with GDP of $53,000 per person.
>
> **[1:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/olympic-medals-and-gdp-per-capita?u=76281980&t=114)** - As you can see, when we're evaluating performance, it is important to adjust for the size of the organization.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/olympic-medals-and-gdp-per-capita?u=76281980&t=121)** With company financial reports, we call this common size financial statements.

> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-income-statement-2?u=76281980&t=103)** What's 1% of 70 billion?
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-income-statement-2?u=76281980&t=106)** That's 700 million dollars.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-income-statement-2?u=76281980&t=108)** Another way to say that is this, by gross margin decreasing by 1.5%, Target's income decreased by over a billion dollars because of that one thing.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-income-statement-2?u=76281980&t=119)** Have we identified something important?
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-income-statement-2?u=76281980&t=121)** Yes, we have, a decrease in gross margin.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-income-statement-2?u=76281980&t=123)** And how long did it take us to do it?
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-income-statement-2?u=76281980&t=125)** Oh, about a minute.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-income-statement-2?u=76281980&t=126)** So look at the powerful analysis that we've done.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-income-statement-2?u=76281980&t=129)** We've taken the income statement of Target, and we've just done a simple thing.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-income-statement-2?u=76281980&t=134)** We divided each number in the income statement by sales for the year, constructing a common size income statement.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-income-statement-2?u=76281980&t=139)** And with that simple tool, in less than a minute, we saw that gross margin is the reason that Target's income decreased from 2012 to 2013.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-income-statement-2?u=76281980&t=151)** We are now prepared to ask some pretty tough questions.

> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/target-s-common-size-balance-sheet-2?u=76281980&t=198)** We can compute individual, specific, pin-pointed ratios to explore in more detail the company's profitability, efficiency, and leverage issues.

> **[1:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/mcdonald-s-most-profitable-items?u=76281980&t=116)** - This sounds like an example of financial ratio analysis.
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/mcdonald-s-most-profitable-items?u=76281980&t=120)** The profitability percentage of different menu items.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/mcdonald-s-most-profitable-items?u=76281980&t=124)** - Exactly.

#### Specific profitability ratios
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=1)** - Now, let's look at some specific profitability ratios, and we'll start right at the top of the income statement with gross profit percentage.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=8)** Gross profit is sales minus cost of goods sold.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=12)** If Nordstrom sells you something for $100 and they pay $65 to buy that thing from their supplier, then Nordstrom's gross profit is $35, and their gross profit percentage is 35%.
>
> **[0:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=25)** The fraction of the selling price that Nordstrom gets to keep right off the top.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=29)** And in a retail organization, or in a manufacturing organization, or an organization that sells a service, you would hope that this gross profit percentage stays stable.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=39)** And we see here with Nordstrom that it's creeping down just a little bit, just a little bit.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=44)** There's a host of possible explanations.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=46)** Maybe the economy is soft, maybe the mix of our customers has changed.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=50)** But we're going to highlight that issue here with gross profit percentage.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=54)** Now, the gross profit percentage is very important, because if you start to have problems there, the only way to make up for that further down in the income statement is by belt tightening.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=64)** Pay our employees less, pay less for electricity, pay less for rent, it's tough.
>
> **[1:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=69)** If the gross profit percentage starts to suffer, it's hard to maintain profits by tightening up on your overhead expenses.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=76)** Let's go down to the income statement one more step and look at operating profit percentage.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=81)** Operating profit is the profit made by a company by doing what it normally does from its operations.
>
> **[1:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=87)** It's gross profit minus those overhead expenses, the selling, general, and administrative expenses, and in a business, we want to see operating profit percentage be stable.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=98)** Here, we see in the case of Nordstrom that the operating profit percentage has slipped down just a little bit from 11.4% to 11.1%, this is something that we want to explore.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=109)** Now there is a ratio that is related to operating profit, and it's called EBITDA, so let's go step by step here, we'll start with the little brother of EBITDA, EBIT.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=119)** EBIT, the acronym, stands for Earnings Before Interest and Taxes.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=124)** It's a synonym for operating income.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=126)** But EBIT sounds much more sophisticated.
>
> **[2:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=129)** EBITDA, the DA part stands for depreciation and amortization.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=133)** These are legitimate business expenses, the wearing out of our machines and our buildings and other assets, but they don't involve cash this year.
>
> **[2:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=142)** So EBITDA can be viewed as an approximation of our operating cash flow, and is a very common measure, EBITDA.
>
> **[2:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=150)** If you're hanging around business people and you say EBITDA, you'll feel immediately like one of the club.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=155)** So a very important ratio is EBITDA divided by sales, and remember, what this reflects is an approximation of operating profitability from a cash standpoint.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=166)** And we would expect our operating profitability always to remain stable.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=170)** Now EBITDA is a very well known number, and I'll just give you a little thing that I learned just a couple of months ago.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=177)** When appraisers are appraising small business, a simple rule of thumb is this, a small business is worth that business's EBITDA multiplied by five.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/specific-profitability-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=187)** Now I'm not giving you appraisal advice here, but EBITDA is such a commonly used number that it's used in appraisals and other things, so make sure that you remember EBITDA, Earnings Before Interest Taxes Depreciation and Amortization.

>
> **Env Vars:** ebitda (11), ebit (3)
> **Code Keywords:** let (3), this, (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (3), is an  (1)

> **Best Practices:** rule of thumb (1)

#### Price-earnings ratio
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's talk about another class of financial ratios, ratios that involve comparing a financial statement number with a market value number.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=9)** And the primary ratio in this category is the P/E ratio, the price-to-earnings ratio.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=16)** This is one of the most famous ratios of all time.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=18)** It's a connection between the amount of net income that a company is generating this year, its earnings, and the price, or the market value that people are willing to pay for that company.
>
> **[0:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=28)** And as you would think, if a company has higher growth prospects in the future, the price-to-earnings ratio is going to be higher because of this reason.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=37)** When you buy a company, are you buying its past or are you buying its future?
>
> **[0:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=42)** You're buying the future.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=43)** And if the future looks very large compared to the earnings right now, you're going to have to pay a premium to buy that company.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=50)** So higher price earnings ratio reflects market expectations that earnings, or net income, in the future will be much higher than they are now.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=57)** Most P/E ratios are between 10 and 30, almost all companies have P/E ratios between 10 and 30, which means this.
>
> **[1:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=65)** If the company has income this year of $1 and the P/E ratio is 10, to buy that company I have to pay $10.
>
> **[1:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=75)** Now sometimes, P/E ratios can get crazy.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=77)** I remember during the internet bubble, back around the year 2000, that P/E ratios sometimes were in excess of $2,000, meaning if you had earnings of $1, people would pay $2,000 to buy that company.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=91)** It was crazy, but what it reflected was people's expectations that earnings at that time were small compared to what earnings were going to be in the future, large expected future growth.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=102)** So the P/E ratio reflects how fast market investors think that a company's earnings will grow in the future.
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=109)** Let's compute the P/E ratio for Nordstrom and for Dillard's.
>
> **[1:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=112)** The computation is simple.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=113)** The P, price, is just market value, the market value of the company.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=118)** Market value of Nordstrom divided by the net income, or earnings, of Nordstrom, is about 15.
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=124)** For Dillard's, it's about 12, so let me stop.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=126)** Let me test your intuition here.
>
> **[2:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=128)** Which company is expected to grow faster in the future?
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=132)** It's Nordstrom.
>
> **[2:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=133)** The higher the P/E ratio, the higher that the market expects this company's growth rate to be in the future.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=139)** So it's a quick reflection of expected future growth rates.
>
> **[2:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=143)** Here's some P/E ratios for companies of which you may have heard.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=148)** And we see that Microsoft and Apple, for example, have very ordinary P/E ratios, 17.
>
> **[2:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=154)** They're great companies, they're beautiful, they're profitable.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=158)** But in terms of expected future growth, which is what P/E ratio reflects, they're average, they're ordinary.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=165)** They're good now and they're going to be good in the future.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=167)** In contrast, Google, with a P/E ratio of 31, is expected to grow at a faster rate in the future than either Microsoft or Apple.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=176)** The champion on this list is Facebook.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=178)** Facebook is a profitable company, but people expect Facebook's earnings to grow really quickly in the future, as reflected in Facebook's P/E ratio of 100.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=189)** I, myself, wish that my personal P/E ratio were about 100.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=192)** This reflects high expected future growth.
>
> **[3:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=195)** Notice that for Citibank and all financial institutions, P/E ratios are low, 10 to 15, because even though those are profitable businesses, the expected future growth is expected to be ordinary or low.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=208)** P/E ratio reflects expected future earnings growth.
>
> **[3:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/price-earnings-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=213)** Profitability ratios, in general, are our way to drill down and figure out why a company is having profitability problems or profitability successes.

#### Number of days' sales in inventory
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=0)** - We buy inventory, and the question is, how long until we sell that inventory?
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=5)** Can we calculate, on average, how long our inventory sits in our store, for example?
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=11)** Well, we have a measure for that.
>
> **[0:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=12)** It's called days' sales in inventory.
>
> **[0:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=15)** And the first step in calculating days' sales in inventory is we take a measure called our inventory turnover, that is, how often do we turn our inventory over every year?
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=26)** Think of it this way, I have inventory.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=29)** How long until I sell it all, buy more, sell it all, buy more, sell it all?
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=35)** How many times do I do that in a year?
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=37)** There's an easy way to calculate that, and that is to take our cost of goods sold and divide that by our average inventory for the year.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=45)** Now, why use average inventory for the year?
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=49)** Well, cost of goods sold occurs throughout the year.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=53)** And our inventory comes and goes throughout the year as well, so to match a cost of goods sold throughout the year measure, we approximate how much inventory do we have on average throughout the year?
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=66)** We'll take our beginning inventory balance, add our ending inventory balance, divide it by two to get an approximation of how much inventory did we have on average throughout the year.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=76)** So to calculate inventory turnover, cost of goods sold divided by average inventory.
>
> **[1:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=81)** Let's go back and look at Nordstrom's again.
>
> **[1:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=84)** Nordstrom's, in 2013, turned their inventory over 5.4 times.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=89)** That was down, it turns out, from 2012, where they turned it over 5.9 times.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=95)** Nordstrom's, 5.4, contrast that with Dillard's in 2013.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=99)** They turned their inventory over three times.
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=103)** Now, we can take that inventory turnover measure and make one more step, and all of the sudden it makes a great deal of sense.
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=111)** When I think about inventory turnover, it's hard for me to get my arms around buying inventory, selling it all, buying inventory, how many times do I do that during a year?
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=119)** If I take that inventory turnover number and simply divide it into 365 days, then I can figure out how long, on average, does my inventory sit here.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=130)** In the case of Nordstrom's, with an inventory turnover of 5.4, if we divide that 5.4 into 365 days, on average, Nordstrom's has its inventory in the store 67.6 days.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=147)** In 2012, it was 62 days.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=149)** So they are up, their average time that inventory sits on the shelf has gone up from 2012 to 2013.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=156)** Compare that, on the other hand, to Dillard's.
>
> **[2:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=159)** If we take their inventory turnover of 3.1 and divide that into 365 days, Dillard's, on average, has its inventory sit there for almost 118 days.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=170)** Nordstrom's, 68 days on average, Dillard's, 118 days on average.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=178)** A big difference there.
>
> **[2:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=179)** Now, it's an important measure because it turns out, if your money is tied up in inventory, you're constrained as to what else you can do with that.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=189)** I'll give you an example related to number of days' sales in inventory.
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=194)** Kay and I teach a case related to Home Depot.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=197)** Back in 1985, Home Depot was almost ready to blink out of existence because of the financial problems they were having.
>
> **[3:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=205)** Their number of days' sales in inventory was 107 days, almost four months!
>
> **[3:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=211)** On average, products would sit in their store for almost four months.
>
> **[3:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=216)** Well, it turns out they had to do something about that.
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=218)** In one year's time, they reduced their days' sales in inventory from 107 days down to 66 days.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=225)** That's amazing!
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=226)** That's one of the reasons Home Depot's still in business is because they were able to make that turn in such a short period of time, down from 107 days to 66.
>
> **[3:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=235)** Well, let's fast-forward to 2014.
>
> **[3:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=239)** It turns out Home Depot has inventory in February of 2014 of $11 billion, and their days' sales in inventory is 78 days.
>
> **[4:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=250)** Now, if Home Depot were to allow its days' sales in inventory to creep up to 107 days, that means their inventory would increase from 11 billion up to $15 billion!
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=264)** And the question is, where would Home Depot get the extra 4 billion in financing?
>
> **[4:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/number-of-days-sales-in-inventory-2?u=76281980&t=268)** Home Depot learned a valuable lesson back in the '80s, keep an eye on how long their inventory sits around, and they were able to squeeze it from four months to down, now, just over two months, saving themselves about $4 billion in the need for external financing.

#### Average collection period
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/average-collection-period-2?u=76281980)

>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/average-collection-period-2?u=76281980&t=6)** We can do the same thing with receivables.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/average-collection-period-2?u=76281980&t=7)** If we sell that inventory on credit, how long until we can expect to get the cash?

#### Length of the operating cycle
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=0)** - One of my favorite things in accounting is to take these two ratios, day sales and inventory and average collection period, and add them together.
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=8)** That'll tell me a company's operating cycle.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=11)** An operating cycle is how long from when we buy the inventory till we collect the cash associated with selling that inventory.

> **[2:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=145)** Turns out, we can calculate that.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=147)** On average, it's about 44 days.
>
> **[2:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=149)** To get the parts for a bike, to put that bike together and sell it to the dealer, 44 days.
>
> **[2:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=155)** How long does the dealer have to pay for that?
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=158)** Well, we can calculate that as well.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=160)** And it turns out it's 17 days.
>
> **[2:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=162)** So, Harley's operating cycle, 61 days.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=165)** 44 days with inventory.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=167)** 17 days with the average collection period.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=170)** Their operating cycle, 61 days.
>
> **[2:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=172)** Now, Harley will also finance the purchase.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=176)** They don't finance the purchase with their dealers, but if a customer comes in, wants a big motorcycle, Harley is more than happy to finance that purchase, and we can calculate, on average, what's their average collection period for those motorcycles that are financed to end customers.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=192)** Turns out, doing the calculations, it's about 1,000 days, which is about 2.7 years.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=198)** On average, when people buy a motorcycle and finance it through Harley, it's about 2.7 years before that motorcycle is finally paid for, on average.
>
> **[3:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=208)** Again, the point here.
>
> **[3:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=210)** I can take day sales and inventory, average collection period to figure out how long from when a company buys their inventory till they collect their cash on that inventory.
>
> **[3:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/length-of-the-operating-cycle-2?u=76281980&t=220)** We call that term, operating cycle.

#### Fixed asset turnover and other utilization ratios
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=0)** - Okay, we've looked at a couple of efficiency measures related to our current assets, days' sales in inventory, average collection period.
>
> **[0:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=7)** Let's turn our attention for a second to our fixed assets, our long-term assets.
>
> **[0:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=13)** Remember why we buy long-term assets, property, plant and equipment?
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=17)** The reason we buy these is to generate sales.
>
> **[0:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=20)** We buy assets to generate sales, we increase our sales in hopes of increasing our income.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=26)** In the case of how efficiently we used our fixed assets, we can actually calculate that.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=30)** There's a measure called fixed-asset turnover which measures the number of dollars in sales generated by each dollars worth of fixed assets.
>
> **[0:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=38)** That's computed by taking sales and dividing it by our average fixed assets.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=43)** Let's illustrate using Nordstrom again.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=45)** In 2013, Nordstrom had generated $4 and 54 cents in sales for every dollar worth of assets they had.
>
> **[0:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=54)** Is that good or bad?
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=55)** Well, let's compare it to 2012.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=57)** In 2012, Nordstrom generated $4 and 81 cents for every dollars worth of fixed assets.
>
> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=64)** So it looks like between 2012 and 2013 Nordstrom got a little less efficient.
>
> **[1:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=71)** But before we panic, let's compare Nordstrom in 2013 with Dillard's in 2013.
>
> **[1:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=77)** Remember, Nordstrom $4 and 54 cents in sales for every dollars worth of fixed assets, Dillard's for the same period of time, generated $3 and three cents in sales for every dollar with a fixed assets.
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=91)** So, based on that, if I were in Dillard's management I would be asking this question, what is Nordstrom doing to generate so many more sales per dollar of fixed assets that we can learn from?
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=105)** Again, the analysis leads us to a question, what are they doing that we need to do?
>
> **[1:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=111)** The analysis doesn't answer the question.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=113)** The analysis often points us in the direction of the next question until we find a person who can help us.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=119)** Now, that's fixed-asset turnover.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=121)** There're other common measures of fixed asset usage and they vary across industry.
>
> **[2:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=126)** For example, in the trucking industry, a common measure of efficiency is miles driven per truck per day.
>
> **[2:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=134)** It makes sense they would track that.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=135)** If the trucks aren't driving, we're not going to be able to generate revenue.
>
> **[2:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=139)** So we've got to have some sort of measure that tracks how efficiently are we using our trucks, miles driven per truck per day is a common measure of efficiency in the trucking industry.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=151)** In manufacturing, a common measure of efficiency there is the capacity utilization rate.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=157)** That is measuring what's our maximum level of output, compared to what we're actually producing.
>
> **[2:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=163)** We can calculate how many, for example, how many cars can we produce a day?
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=168)** And then how many are we producing a day?
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=171)** That'll tell us our capacity utilization rate.
>
> **[2:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=174)** Other efficiency measures.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=176)** Nordstrom for example, they had $474 in sales per square foot of store in 2013.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=184)** They will keep track of that.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=185)** That was up from 470 the year before.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=188)** Home Depot does the same thing.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=190)** In 2013, Home Depot generated $334 in sales for every square foot of store.
>
> **[3:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=198)** Other measures, sales per employee, WhatsApp in 2013 $7 million in sales per employee, Apple in 2013 $2 million in sales per employee, Walmart for the same period of time $235,000 in sales per employee.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=217)** This tells us just as another measure, a demonstration of a measure of efficiency.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=222)** Other examples, the employee utilization rate, for example, this is common in consulting firms, accounting firms, legal firms, how many hours can we have an individual work and how many hours are we billing for that individual?
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=236)** The dream, they can work 40 hours hours a week and we're billing them for 40 hours a week.
>
> **[4:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=243)** Do we want to have that utilization rate be 100% every week all the time?
>
> **[4:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=248)** Or if they're working 60 hours a week, do we want 100% utilization rate?
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=253)** We can use that measure to determine how much are we working our employees and are we in danger of pushing 'em a little too hard so that we don't have that problem anymore when they leave and go somewhere else.
>
> **[4:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=264)** If we're being too efficient, we may overwork somebody there.
>
> **[4:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=267)** We've got to keep track of our efficiency.
>
> **[4:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=269)** Again, it's one component of the DuPont framework.
>
> **[4:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/fixed-asset-turnover-and-other-utilization-ratios?u=76281980&t=273)** We can become a much more profitable firm in terms of return on equity by making sure that we are as efficient with our assets as is possible.

### 7. Leverage Ratios

#### Singapore Airlines' low leverage

>
> **[0:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/singapore-airlines-low-leverage?u=76281980&t=47)** - Yeah, the most profitable by far.

> **[1:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/singapore-airlines-low-leverage?u=76281980&t=64)** - Ah, you're forgetting the DuPont framework!
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/singapore-airlines-low-leverage?u=76281980&t=66)** - D'oh, you're right!
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/singapore-airlines-low-leverage?u=76281980&t=68)** A key insight of the DuPont framework is that return on equity has three dimensions, profitability, efficiency, and leverage.
>
> **[1:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/singapore-airlines-low-leverage?u=76281980&t=76)** - Correct, even though Singapore Airlines had excellent profitability in 2007, the company also had very low leverage.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/singapore-airlines-low-leverage?u=76281980&t=83)** This combination of high profitability and low leverage resulted in an ordinary return on equity of just 14%.
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/singapore-airlines-low-leverage?u=76281980&t=90)** - Leverage, an important element in a company's return on equity.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/singapore-airlines-low-leverage?u=76281980&t=95)** Let's talk about some leverage measures.
