#### Current ratio
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=0)** - Step one in a financial analysis is computing return on equity and then the DuPont framework analysis to look at the profitability, efficiency and leverage components.
>
> **[0:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=10)** We're now going to hone in on the leverage component of return on equity with some specific ratios.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=16)** Let's start with current ratio.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=18)** Current ratio is a measure of liquidity.
>
> **[0:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=21)** Liquidity reflects the ability of a company to pay its obligations in the short-term.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=26)** Short-term, we typically define as less than one year.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=30)** Current ratio is computed as current assets divided by current liabilities.
>
> **[0:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=34)** And let me remind you what a current asset is and what a current liability is.
>
> **[0:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=39)** A current asset is an asset expected to be used or turned into cash within one year.
>
> **[0:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=45)** So, for example, accounts receivable, that's a current asset, because we expect those accounts to be collected in cash within one year.
>
> **[0:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=53)** Inventory is a current asset because we expect that inventory to be sold and then the cash collected all within one year.
>
> **[1:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=61)** Land is not a current asset, typically, because if we come back a year from now, we expect that land to still be there.
>
> **[1:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=67)** Current asset.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=68)** A cash is also a great current asset 'cause already is cash.
>
> **[1:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=72)** So our current assets the liquid assets, the ones that we expect to become cash soon in less than one year.
>
> **[1:19](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=79)** Similarly, current liabilities are the liabilities that we expect to have to pay within one year.
>
> **[1:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=85)** Accounts payable is a good example of a current liability.
>
> **[1:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=88)** We're going to pay our suppliers what we owe them within one year.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=92)** So the current ratio reflects the balance between the assets that we have that are going to become cash within one year and the liabilities that we have that we're going to have to pay within one year.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=102)** And we like to see a bit of a cushion there.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=104)** In fact, it's very common in bank loan contracts that a bank will say to a borrower, "Your current ratio has to stay above a certain level, "above 1.5 or above two.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=115)** "And if you fall below two, "we start to get nervous.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=118)** "Maybe you're going to be able to pay us "when you are supposed to pay us.
>
> **[2:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=121)** "And so your loan is in default."
>
> **[2:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=124)** So the rule of thumb is current ratios should be be greater than two but that's an old rule of thumb.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=130)** In the new world that we have now, the technology world, companies are able to manage their current assets much more efficiently.
>
> **[2:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=137)** Companies don't need as much inventory as they used to need because their information systems can track their inventory very carefully and so companies don't need to have as much extra inventory lying around.
>
> **[2:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=148)** Cash can be managed more tightly.
>
> **[2:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=151)** Accounts receivable can be tracked more precisely.
>
> **[2:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=153)** So in recent years, current ratios have slipped below two.
>
> **[2:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=158)** In fact, you see a list of very safe, financially safe companies here, all with current ratios less than two.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=165)** That's normal these days.
>
> **[2:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=166)** So the old rule of thumb, the rule that your mom and dad learned when they went to school was current ratio should be about two.
>
> **[2:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=173)** Current ratios are often less than two now.
>
> **[2:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=176)** But in general, remember that the current ratio reflects liquidity, the ability of a company to pay its debts in a short-term and we like to see that steady.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/current-ratio-2?u=76281980&t=186)** If that starts to slip in any given company, we get nervous about that company's ability to pay its debts in a short-term.

>
> **Definitions:** is a  (3), is an  (1)
> **Code Keywords:** let (2), default. (1)
> **Best Practices:** rule of thumb (3)
> **Versions:** 1.5 (1)

#### Leverage ratios
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=1)** - So let's look at some specific measures of leverage, measures that are commonly used.
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=6)** Investors put in their investment in the company.
>
> **[0:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=9)** But if that investment is not enough to buy all of the assets they need to fulfill their business objectives, they need to borrow money, they need to leverage their investment.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=18)** That makes the business larger with the same initial shareholder investment.
>
> **[0:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=22)** That's reflected in the assets-to-equity ratio, a measure of leverage.
>
> **[0:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=26)** With more leverage, a company's return on equity can be higher even with the same amount of shareholder investment.
>
> **[0:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=35)** Debt ratio.
>
> **[0:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=36)** Very simple measure.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=37)** Total liabilities divided by total assets, the fraction of financing that was obtained through borrowing.
>
> **[0:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=44)** We see for Nordstrom about 75%, for Dillard's about 50%.
>
> **[0:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=49)** What does that 75% mean?
>
> **[0:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=51)** Of all the money that Nordstrom needed to buy its assets, it borrowed about 75%.
>
> **[0:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=57)** Dillard's borrowed about 50%.
>
> **[1:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=60)** The rule of thumb is that for large companies, a debt ratio is usually between 50 and 60%.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=66)** So when I see that 50% debt ratio for Dillard's, I think, fine, they're normal.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=70)** They've leveraged, they've borrowed about the same amount as most companies borrow.
>
> **[1:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=74)** When I see that debt ratio of Nordstrom, on the other hand, of 75%, I now have some questions.
>
> **[1:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=80)** I'd like this chief financial officer, the CFO, for Nordstrom to come here and tell me what was the business decision that you made so that you have so much leverage?
>
> **[1:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=90)** Another measure of leverage is called the debt-to-equity ratio, total liabilities divided by total shareholder investment.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=98)** We see that number for Nordstrom is about three, for Dillard's it's about one.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=102)** And at this point, we need to stop, because I'm sure you're wondering, Wait a second, what is the measure of leverage?
>
> **[1:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=109)** Because we saw in the DuPont framework assets-equity ratio, we just saw the debt ratio, we just see here debt-to-equity ratio.
>
> **[1:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=117)** Which is the one or how are they different?
>
> **[2:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=120)** Well, it turns out they're all related to the same underlying data.
>
> **[2:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=123)** Let me show that to you with a simple numerical example.
>
> **[2:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=127)** We got a simple company, investors invest a dollar.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=130)** They then go out and borrow a dollar, and with those $2 can now buy $2 of assets.
>
> **[2:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=135)** $2 of assets coming from $1 of borrowing liability and $1 of owner investment.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=141)** Let's now compute three different measures of leverage using this same common set of data.
>
> **[2:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=147)** The debt ratio, $1 of liabilities divided by $2 of assets, 50%.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=152)** Half of the financing has come through borrowing.
>
> **[2:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=156)** The debt-to-equity ratio, total liabilities divided by total equity is one.
>
> **[2:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=160)** It's one to one.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=161)** We have exactly as much borrowing as we do shareholder investment.
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=165)** The assets-to-equity ratio is two.
>
> **[2:47](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=167)** Total assets is two.
>
> **[2:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=169)** Shareholder investment is one.
>
> **[2:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=171)** We have twice as many assets as we could buy with our own invested money.
>
> **[2:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=175)** That means we had to leverage our investment.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=177)** You see that there are three different measures of the same underlying leverage.
>
> **[3:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=181)** Why do have three?
>
> **[3:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=183)** Well, it's just a matter of taste.
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=185)** People use different ratios.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=186)** They all reflect the same thing.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=188)** So how do we know which one to use?
>
> **[3:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=191)** Well, the way you know is ask the person on the other side of the table, which leverage ratio are you using?
>
> **[3:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=196)** Let's say that somebody tells you that the leverage ratio is 0.5, what does that mean?
>
> **[3:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=201)** Well, are they talking about the debt ratio?
>
> **[3:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=203)** If they're talking about the debt ratio, a debt ratio of 0.5 means that half of the total financing has been borrowed.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=209)** 50% of total financing is borrowed, total liabilities divided by total assets.
>
> **[3:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=215)** Or are they referring to the debt-to-equity ratio?
>
> **[3:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=218)** That's 0.5.
>
> **[3:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=219)** Well, then that means that the borrowing is half as much as the shareholder investment.
>
> **[3:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=224)** That works out to be this.
>
> **[3:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=225)** 1/3 of the financing has come from borrowing and 2/3, twice as much, has come from shareholder investment.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=232)** What does that mean if the assets-to-equity ratio is 0.5?
>
> **[3:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=236)** It means shareholders have invested money, the managers of the company have ruined half of those assets, and now the assets are only half as much as what the shareholders originally put in.
>
> **[4:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=246)** So you can see that a leveraged ratio or 0.5, what does that mean?
>
> **[4:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=249)** It depends on the scale.
>
> **[4:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=251)** So when you're talking with somebody and they say the leverage ratio is 0.5, you need to stop them right there and say, Wait, are you talking about debt ratio, debt-to-equity ratio, or assets-to-equity ratio?
>
> **[4:22](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=262)** Now, one common feature of all these measures of leverage is that they all come from balance sheet numbers: assets, liabilities, owner investment.
>
> **[4:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=271)** There also a very important income-statement-focused measure of leverage, and this is called times interest earned.
>
> **[4:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=277)** These numbers come from the income statement.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=279)** It's operating income divided by the amount of interest expense.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=283)** And we see here that for both Nordstrom and Dillard's, the numbers are about eight, a very good number.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=290)** What it reflects is this.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=291)** Both of these companies are generating, operating profits eight times as much as they need to be able to pay their interest.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=297)** That's a very safe cushion.
>
> **[5:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=300)** If you're a banker, thinking of loaning money to Nordstrom or Dillard's, you think, Okay, they're safe, they're generating plenty of operating profit to be able to pay their interest to me.
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=309)** So the times interest earned ratio is an income-statement-focused measure of leverage.
>
> **[5:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=315)** Leverage, an important component of increasing a company's return on equity.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=321)** We saw in the DuPont framework that there are benefits to leverage.
>
> **[5:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=325)** More leverage means larger assets.
>
> **[5:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=327)** More assets, more sales.
>
> **[5:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=329)** More sales, more income with that same initial owner investment.
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=333)** So the benefit of leverage is we can increase the size of our business and increase our profits by using somebody else's money.
>
> **[5:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=341)** That's the benefit of leverage.
>
> **[5:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=343)** There are also risks associated with leverage.
>
> **[5:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=346)** Once you borrow money, you now have the relentless fixed cost of that interest.
>
> **[5:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=350)** And whether things are going well or going poorly, you still have to pay that interest.
>
> **[5:54](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=354)** What that means is if your business experience is a short-term downturn, you may not live through that short-term downturn because you have to pay your interest.
>
> **[6:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=364)** You could go into bankruptcy because you can no longer pay your interest.
>
> **[6:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=368)** And are those borrowed resources being used efficiently to generate enough profit to be able to pay the interest on the borrowed money?
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=376)** If not so, you're digging yourself deeper and deeper into a financial hole.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=381)** There are benefits to leverage, there are risks to leverage.
>
> **[6:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/leverage-ratios-2?u=76281980&t=385)** Prudent leverage in a business can increase the return on equity, but leverage has to be used prudently.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Code Keywords:** let (4), this. (2)
> **Versions:** 0.5 (6)
> **Definitions:** is called (2), means that (1), is an  (1), is a  (1)
> **Env Vars:** cfo (1)
> **Best Practices:** rule of thumb (1)
> **Speakers:** - so (1)

#### Excess data and comparability issues
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=0)** - Okay, we have to be careful, now.
>
> **[0:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=2)** There are potential pitfalls associated with financial ratio analysis, and we've got to be aware of those pitfalls so that we can carefully do our analysis and carefully apply our results.
>
> **[0:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=14)** I think of four things right off the top of my head.
>
> **[0:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=17)** First is, financial information is not all the value-relevant information that's available about a firm.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=24)** Secondly, financial statements from different companies are sometimes not comparable.
>
> **[0:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=29)** We'll look at a couple of examples of that.
>
> **[0:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=31)** Third common mistake is, we always seem to be searching for one problem, when it could be that there's a multitude of reasons a company is doing very well or doing very poorly.
>
> **[0:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=43)** And then, the fourth common mistake with financial ratio analysis is we weight information differently.
>
> **[0:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=48)** We either look at history too much, the distant past too much, or we anchor on the recent past.
>
> **[0:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=55)** We've got to be careful when we're using information to apply it appropriately.
>
> **[0:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=58)** Let's take a look at the first one, financial statements don't contain all value-relevant information.
>
> **[1:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=63)** It turns out, financial statements are only one part of the information spectrum.
>
> **[1:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=68)** There's lots of information available about a company out there, and while financial information is relevant and reliable, it's not everything, and the danger is, the precision of the financial statement numbers can be very misleading.
>
> **[1:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=83)** We may think it's more important than it is because there are numbers attached to it, so we got to be very careful when using financial information to not overweight it in terms of its importance.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=93)** For example, for publicly traded companies in the United States, company's stock prices fluctuate every day in response to all sorts of news.
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=102)** Economy-wide news, industry news, company-specific news.
>
> **[1:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=106)** Every day.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=108)** And yet, financial statements come out once every three months for publicly traded companies.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=113)** We've got to make sure that we incorporate all information, and not overweight the financial information.
>
> **[1:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=119)** As an example, one of my favorite examples, this one goes down in accounting history.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=125)** In January of 2001, AOL, formerly known as America Online, acquired Time Warner.
>
> **[2:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=132)** On the transaction date, an asset labeled goodwill was recorded for the seemingly precise amount of 128 billion dollars.
>
> **[2:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=141)** In other words, when AOL bought Time Warner, they recorded on their books 128 billion dollars worth of goodwill, an exact number of 128 billion.
>
> **[2:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=152)** And within two years, it was determined that that asset was worth zero.
>
> **[2:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=157)** Turns out, they missed it by 128 billion dollars.
>
> **[2:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=161)** But when they initially recorded it, there was a lot of "wow, 128 billion dollars."
>
> **[2:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=165)** It turns out, two years later, it wasn't there.
>
> **[2:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=168)** We've got to be very careful.
>
> **[2:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=170)** Sometimes, financial information with its precision can lead us to assume that it's more important than it is.
>
> **[2:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=177)** The second one.
>
> **[2:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=178)** Financial statements from different companies are sometimes not comparable.
>
> **[3:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=182)** They may classify items differently.
>
> **[3:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=184)** They may use different accounting methods or assumptions, or we may have a business that's a conglomeration of a variety of business segments.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=192)** For example, let's look at Exxon Mobil, a big oil company, and Gazprom, the biggest natural gas company in the world.
>
> **[3:20](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=200)** If we were to look at their report at operating cash flows, we would conclude that Gazprom, based in Russia, generated more cash flow from operations than did Exxon Mobil.
>
> **[3:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=209)** By the way, those numbers, 53 billion dollars for Gazprom.
>
> **[3:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=214)** 45 billion dollars for Exxon, wow.
>
> **[3:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=217)** But, turns out, Gazprom was using international accounting standards.
>
> **[3:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=222)** Exxon Mobil was using the accounting standards applicable in the United States.
>
> **[3:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=226)** When we make those two financial statements or those two numbers comparable, we have to make some adjustments.
>
> **[3:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=232)** It turns out, comparing operating cash flows on a consistent basis, Exxon Mobil generated 70 billion dollars in operating cash flows.
>
> **[4:02](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=242)** Gazprom, closer to 60 billion dollars.
>
> **[4:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=245)** So, before we do comparisons of two financial statements, two companies, we've got to make sure that it's apples and apples.
>
> **[4:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=253)** We've got to be very careful.
>
> **[4:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=255)** In addition, not only were we comparing international rules with U.S. rules in that example, but we've got to pay attention to how companies classify certain accounts on their financial statements.
>
> **[4:26](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=266)** Nordstrom's, for example, when we were comparing Nordstrom's with Dillard's, Nordstrom's has substantial interest income, because remember, they have their own accounts receivable.
>
> **[4:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=275)** Dillard's had farmed that out with an alliance with General Electric.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=280)** They had interest income, but it was less, and the reason was because General Electric was taking a share for administering Dillard's accounts receivable.
>
> **[4:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=291)** We've got to make sure when we do comparisons that it's apples and apples when it comes to accounting methods.
>
> **[4:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=297)** Let's take an example we're all familiar with, Berkshire Hathaway.
>
> **[5:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=301)** If we were going to compare Berkshire Hathaway's financial statements to another company's financial statements and do our ratio analysis, who would we compare them to?
>
> **[5:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=309)** Think about Berkshire Hathaway for a moment.
>
> **[5:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=311)** What does that company consist of?
>
> **[5:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=314)** Well, Burlington Northern Railroad, Fruit of the Loom, Geico Insurance, Dairy Queen, RHC Wille, a furniture company, C's Candy.
>
> **[5:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=321)** Worldbook Encyclopedia, Geranimals, Walls Lamont, they make gloves, Benjamin Moore, paint, and a whole bunch of other companies.
>
> **[5:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=330)** Who would we compare Berkshire Hathaway to if we were going to do a comparison?
>
> **[5:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=333)** I can't think of anybody.
>
> **[5:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=335)** So we've got to be careful when we're comparing one company with another, that not only are they in the same line of business, but they use the same accounting methods and they classify items on their financial statements the same.
>
> **[5:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/excess-data-and-comparability-issues?u=76281980&t=349)** We've just got to be careful when doing comparisons.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Warnings:** be careful (4), common mistake (2), be aware (1)
> **CLI Commands:** make (6)
> **Code Keywords:** let (3)
> **Env Vars:** aol (2), rhc (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (3)
> **Definitions:** known as (1), in other words (1)

#### "Smoking gun" and historical data biases
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/smoking-gun-and-historical-data-biases?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>

#### What financial ratio analysis can NOT do
> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=0)** - All right, let's be clear about what financial ratio analysis cannot do.
>
> **[0:04](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=4)** - First, financial ratio analysis can't do anything if the underlined financial statement data are not reliable.
>
> **[0:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=11)** - If you are doing an important analysis make sure the data come from a reliable source.
>
> **[0:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=16)** If the data come from a person you can trust, good.
>
> **[0:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=18)** If you aren't sure about the source it's a good idea to have the numbers checked by an outside party, an auditor.
>
> **[0:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=24)** - For example, a friend of mine asked me to analyze the numbers of a business he was thinking of buying.
>
> **[0:30](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=30)** It didn't take me long to realize that the financial numbers for this family business were hopelessly mixed in with the personal family expenses.
>
> **[0:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=37)** Such as: gas and utilities and groceries.
>
> **[0:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=40)** It was impossible to figure out how much income the business was actually generating.
>
> **[0:46](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=46)** Without reliable data it was extremely risky to try to evaluate the business.
>
> **[0:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=50)** In the end my friend decided not to buy the business.
>
> **[0:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=52)** - It turns out with unreliable numbers not even the most sophisticated financial analysis can tell you anything.
>
> **[0:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=59)** - Next, financial ratio analysis cannot help you pick winning and losing stocks in the stock market.
>
> **[1:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=66)** - Market prices move too quickly to reflect the most current information about companies.
>
> **[1:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=70)** When I say quickly, I mean ten to fifteen minutes.
>
> **[1:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=73)** - So it's highly unlikely that you, using financial statement data that are weeks or months old, are going to be able to pick winning and losing stocks that have not already been identified and traded on by the market traders who are continuously doing that ever single day.
>
> **[1:29](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=89)** - If you want to invest in the stock market our recommendation is to make long term investments and diversify portfolios such as index funds.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=97)** But that's a topic for another time.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=99)** - Lastly, financial ratio analysis can't make your decisions for you.
>
> **[1:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=104)** Yes, the ratios can identify very bad companies and very good companies.
>
> **[1:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=108)** But many companies, most companies, lie in that gray area in the middle.
>
> **[1:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=113)** With some strengths and some weaknesses.
>
> **[1:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=115)** - Financial ratio analysis helps you understand companies.
>
> **[1:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=118)** But in the end you will need to use your understanding of the numbers to then talk with people to find out exactly what is going on.
>
> **[2:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=125)** - Your financial analysis puts you in the right room, with the right people, asking the right questions.
>
> **[2:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/running-a-profitable-business-understanding-financial-ratios/what-financial-ratio-analysis-can-not-do?u=76281980&t=131)** But in the end you'll need to use your own business judgment in making decisions.
